515<h3 class="no_margin">

690<div id="Abs1" lang="en" class="tsec sec">

691<h2 class="head no_bottom_margin" id="Abs1title">

692Abstract</h2>

693<!--article-meta-->

694<div>

695<p id="__p1" class="p p-first">

696Cannabidiol (CBD), a <em>

697Cannabis sativa</em>

698 constituent, is a pharmacologically broad-spectrum drug that in recent years has drawn increasing interest as a treatment for a range of neuropsychiatric disorders. The purpose of the current review is to determine CBD&#x02019;s potential as a treatment for anxiety-related disorders, by assessing evidence from preclinical, human experimental, clinical, and epidemiological studies. We found that existing preclinical evidence strongly supports CBD as a treatment for generalized anxiety disorder, panic disorder, social anxiety disorder, obsessive&#x02013;compulsive disorder, and post-traumatic stress disorder when administered acutely; however, few studies have investigated chronic CBD dosing. Likewise, evidence from human studies supports an anxiolytic role of CBD, but is currently limited to acute dosing, also with few studies in clinical populations. Overall, current evidence indicates CBD has considerable potential as a treatment for multiple anxiety disorders, with need for further study of chronic and therapeutic effects in relevant clinical populations.</p>

699<div id="__sec1" class="sec">

700<h3 id="__sec1title">

701Electronic supplementary material</h3>

702<p id="__p2" class="p p-first-last">

703The online version of this article (doi:10.1007/s13311-015-0387-1) contains supplementary material, which is available to authorized users.</p>

704</div>

705</div>

706<div class="sec">

707<strong class="kwd-title">

708Keywords: </strong>

709<span class="kwd-text">

710Cannabidiol, Endocannabinoids, Anxiety, Generalized anxiety disorder, Post-traumatic stress disorder</span>

711</div>

712</div>

713<div id="Sec1" class="tsec sec">

714<h2 class="head no_bottom_margin" id="Sec1title">

715Introduction</h2>

716<p id="__p3" class="p p-first">

717Fear and anxiety are adaptive responses essential to coping with threats to survival. Yet excessive or persistent fear may be maladaptive, leading to disability. Symptoms arising from excessive fear and anxiety occur in a number of neuropsychiatric disorders, including generalized anxiety disorder (GAD), panic disorder (PD), post-traumatic stress disorder (PTSD), social anxiety disorder (SAD), and obsessive&#x02013;compulsive disorder (OCD). Notably, PTSD and OCD are no longer classified as anxiety disorders in the recent revision of the Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders-5; however, excessive anxiety is central to the symptomatology of both disorders. These anxiety-related disorders are associated with a diminished sense of well-being, elevated rates of unemployment and relationship breakdown, and elevated suicide risk [<a href="#CR1" rid="CR1" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459628">

7181</a>

719&#x02013;<a href="#CR3" rid="CR3" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459541">

7203</a>

721]. Together, they have a lifetime prevalence in the USA of 29&#x000a0;% [<a href="#CR4" rid="CR4" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459534">

7224</a>

723], the highest of any mental disorder, and constitute an immense social and economic burden [<a href="#CR5" rid="CR5" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459515">

7245</a>

725, <a href="#CR6" rid="CR6" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459549">

7266</a>

727].</p>

728<p id="__p4">

729Currently available pharmacological treatments include serotonin reuptake inhibitors, serotonin&#x02013;norepinephrine reuptake inhibitors, benzodiazepines, monoamine oxidase inhibitors, tricyclic antidepressant drugs, and partial 5-hydroxytryptamine (5-HT)<sub>

7301A</sub>

731 receptor agonists. Anticonvulsants and atypical antipsychotics are also used to treat PTSD. These medications are associated with limited response rates and residual symptoms, particularly in PTSD, and adverse effects may also limit tolerability and adherence [<a href="#CR7" rid="CR7" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_579828133">

7327</a>

733&#x02013;<a href="#CR10" rid="CR10" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459566">

73410</a>

735]. The substantial burden of anxiety-related disorders and the limitations of current treatments place a high priority on developing novel pharmaceutical treatments.</p>

736<p id="__p5" class="p p-last">

737Cannabidiol (CBD) is a phytocannabinoid constituent of <em>

738Cannabis sativa</em>

739 that lacks the psychoactive effects of &#x02206;<sup>

7409-</sup>

741tetrahydrocannabinol (THC). CBD has broad therapeutic properties across a range of neuropsychiatric disorders, stemming from diverse central nervous system actions [<a href="#CR11" rid="CR11" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459513">

74211</a>

743, <a href="#CR12" rid="CR12" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459576">

74412</a>

745]. In recent years, CBD has attracted increasing interest as a potential anxiolytic treatment [<a href="#CR13" rid="CR13" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459611">

74613</a>

747&#x02013;<a href="#CR15" rid="CR15" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459607">

74815</a>

749]. The purpose of this review is to assess evidence from current preclinical, clinical, and epidemiological studies pertaining to the potential risks and benefits of CBD as a treatment for anxiety disorders.</p>

750</div>

751<div id="Sec2" class="tsec sec">

752<h2 class="head no_bottom_margin" id="Sec2title">

753Methods</h2>

754<p id="__p6" class="p p-first-last">

755A search of MEDLINE (PubMed), PsycINFO, Web of Science Scopus, and the Cochrane Library databases was conducted for English-language papers published up to 1 January 2015, using the search terms &#x0201c;cannabidiol&#x0201d; and &#x0201c;anxiety&#x0201d; or &#x0201c;fear&#x0201d; or &#x0201c;stress&#x0201d; or &#x0201c;anxiety disorder&#x0201d; or &#x0201c;generalized anxiety disorder&#x0201d; or &#x0201c;social anxiety disorder&#x0201d; or &#x0201c;social phobia&#x0201d; or &#x0201c;post-traumatic stress disorder&#x0201d; or &#x0201c;panic disorder&#x0201d; or &#x0201c;obsessive compulsive disorder&#x0201d;. In total, 49 primary preclinical, clinical, or epidemiological studies were included. Neuroimaging studies that documented results from anxiety-related tasks, or resting neural activity, were included. Epidemiological or clinical studies that assessed CBD&#x02019;s effects on anxiety symptoms, or the potential protective effects of CBD on anxiety symptoms induced by cannabis use (where the CBD content of cannabis is inferred via a higher CBD:THC ratio), were included.</p>

756</div>

757<div id="Sec3" class="tsec sec">

758<h2 class="head no_bottom_margin" id="Sec3title">

759CBD Pharmacology Relevant to Anxiety</h2>

760<div id="Sec4" class="sec sec-first">

761<h3 id="Sec4title">

762General Pharmacology and Therapeutic Profile</h3>

763<p id="__p7" class="p p-first">

764<em>

765Cannabis sativa</em>

766, a species of the <em>

767Cannabis</em>

768 genus of flowering plants, is one of the most frequently used illicit recreational substances in Western culture. The 2 major phyto- cannabinoid constituents with central nervous system activity are THC, responsible for the euphoric and mind-altering effects, and CBD, which lacks these psychoactive effects. Preclinical and clinical studies show CBD possesses a wide range of therapeutic properties, including antipsychotic, analgesic, neuroprotective, anticonvulsant, antiemetic, antioxidant, anti-inflammatory, antiarthritic, and antineoplastic properties (see [<a href="#CR11" rid="CR11" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459536">

76911</a>

770, <a href="#CR12" rid="CR12" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459585">

77112</a>

772, <a href="#CR16" rid="CR16" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459567">

77316</a>

774&#x02013;<a href="#CR19" rid="CR19" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_579828123">

77519</a>

776] for reviews). A review of potential side effects in humans found that CBD was well tolerated across a wide dose range, up to 1500&#x000a0;mg/day (orally), with no reported psychomotor slowing, negative mood effects, or vital sign abnormalities noted [<a href="#CR20" rid="CR20" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459631">

77720</a>

778].</p>

779<p id="__p8" class="p p-last">

780CBD has a broad pharmacological profile, including interactions with several receptors known to regulate fear and anxiety-related behaviors, specifically the cannabinoid type 1 receptor (CB<sub>

7811</sub>

782R), the serotonin 5-HT<sub>

7831A</sub>

784 receptor, and the transient receptor potential (TRP) vanilloid type 1 (TRPV1) receptor [<a href="#CR11" rid="CR11" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459599">

78511</a>

786,&#x000a0;<a href="#CR12" rid="CR12" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459580">

78712</a>

788, <a href="#CR19" rid="CR19" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_579828127">

78919</a>

790, <a href="#CR21" rid="CR21" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459658">

79121</a>

792]. In addition, CBD may also regulate, directly or indirectly, the peroxisome proliferator-activated receptor-&#x003b3;, the orphan G-protein-coupled receptor 55, the equilibrative nucleoside transporter, the adenosine transporter, additional TRP channels, and glycine receptors [<a href="#CR11" rid="CR11" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459647">

79311</a>

794,&#x000a0;<a href="#CR12" rid="CR12" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459640">

79512</a>

796, <a href="#CR19" rid="CR19" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_579828128">

79719</a>

798, <a href="#CR21" rid="CR21" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459592">

79921</a>

800]. In the current review of primary studies, the following receptor-specific actions were found to have been investigated as potential mediators of CBD&#x02019;s anxiolytic action: CB<sub>

8011</sub>

802R, TRPV1 receptors, and 5-HT<sub>

8031A</sub>

804 receptors. Pharmacology relevant to these actions is detailed below.</p>

805</div>

806<div id="Sec5" class="sec">

807<h3 id="Sec5title">

808The Endocannabinoid System</h3>

809<p id="__p9" class="p p-first">

810Following cloning of the endogenous receptor for THC, namely the CB<sub>

8111</sub>

812R, endogenous CB<sub>

8131</sub>

814R ligands, or &#x0201c;endocannabinoids&#x0201d; (eCBs) were discovered, namely anandamide (AEA) and 2-arachidonoylglycerol (reviewed in [<a href="#CR22" rid="CR22" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459527">

81522</a>

816]). The CB<sub>

8171</sub>

818R is an inhibitory G<sub>

819i/o</sub>

820 protein-coupled receptor that is mainly localized to nerve terminals, and is expressed on both &#x003b3;-aminobutryic acid-ergic and glutamatergic neurons. eCBs are fatty acid derivatives that are synthesized on demand in response to neuronal depolarization and Ca<sup>

8212+</sup>

822 influx, via cleavage of membrane phospholipids. The primary mechanism by which eCBs regulate synaptic function is retrograde signaling, wherein eCBs produced by depolarization of the postsynaptic neuron activate presynaptic CB<sub>

8231</sub>

824Rs, leading to inhibition of neurotransmitter release [<a href="#CR23" rid="CR23" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459660">

82523</a>

826]. The &#x0201c;eCB system&#x0201d; includes AEA and 2-arachidonoylglycerol; their respective degradative enzymes fatty acid amide hydroxylase (FAAH) and monoacylglycerol lipase; the&#x000a0;CB<sub>

8271</sub>

828R and related CB<sub>

8292</sub>

830 receptor (the latter expressed mainly in the periphery); as well as several other receptors activated by eCBs, including the TRPV1 receptor, peroxisome proliferator-activated receptor-&#x003b3;, and G protein-coupled 55 receptor, which functionally interact with CB<sub>

8311</sub>

832R signaling (reviewed in [<a href="#CR21" rid="CR21" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459646">

83321</a>

834, <a href="#CR24" rid="CR24" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459608">

83524</a>

836]). Interactions with the TRPV1 receptor, in particular, appear to be critical in regulating the extent to which eCB release leads to inhibition or facilitation of presynaptic neurotransmitter release [<a href="#CR25" rid="CR25" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459656">

83725</a>

838]. The TRPV1 receptor is a postsynaptic cation channel that underlies sensation of noxious heat in the periphery,&#x000a0;with capsacin (hot chili) as an exogenous ligand. TRPV1 receptors are also expressed in the brain, including the amygdala, periaqueductal grey, hippocampus, and other areas [<a href="#CR26" rid="CR26" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459612">

83926</a>

840, <a href="#CR27" rid="CR27" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459641">

84127</a>

842].</p>

843<p id="__p10">

844The eCB system regulates diverse physiological functions, including caloric energy balance and immune function [<a href="#CR28" rid="CR28" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459601">

84528</a>

846]. The eCB system is also integral to regulation of emotional behavior, being essential to forms of synaptic plasticity that determine learning and response to emotionally salient, particularly highly aversive events [<a href="#CR29" rid="CR29" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459671">

84729</a>

848, <a href="#CR30" rid="CR30" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459615">

84930</a>

850]. Activation of CB<sub>

8511</sub>

852Rs produces anxiolytic effects in various models of unconditioned fear, relevant to multiple anxiety disorder symptom domains (reviewed in [<a href="#CR30" rid="CR30" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459548">

85330</a>

854&#x02013;<a href="#CR33" rid="CR33" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459520">

85533</a>

856]). Regarding conditioned fear, the effect of CB<sub>

8571</sub>

858R activation is complex: CB<sub>

8591</sub>

860R activation may enhance or reduce fear expression, depending on brain locus and the eCB ligand [<a href="#CR34" rid="CR34" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459550">

86134</a>

862]; however, CB<sub>

8631</sub>

864R activation potently enhances fear extinction [<a href="#CR35" rid="CR35" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459532">

86535</a>

866], and can prevent fear reconsolidation. Genetic manipulations that impede CB<sub>

8671</sub>

868R activation are anxiogenic [<a href="#CR35" rid="CR35" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459633">

86935</a>

870], and individuals with eCB system gene polymorphisms that reduce eCB tone&#x02014;for example, FAAH gene polymorphisms&#x02014;exhibit physiological, psychological, and neuroimaging features consistent with impaired fear regulation [<a href="#CR36" rid="CR36" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459674">

87136</a>

872]. Reduction of AEA&#x02013;CB<sub>

8731</sub>

874R signaling in the amygdala mediates the anxiogenic effects of corticotropin-releasing hormone [<a href="#CR37" rid="CR37" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459556">

87537</a>

876], and CB<sub>

8771</sub>

878R activation is essential to negative feedback of the neuroendocrine stress response, and protects against the adverse effects of chronic stress [<a href="#CR38" rid="CR38" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459505">

87938</a>

880, <a href="#CR39" rid="CR39" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459523">

88139</a>

882]. Finally, chronic stress impairs eCB signaling in the hippocampus and amygdala, leading to anxiety [<a href="#CR40" rid="CR40" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459522">

88340</a>

884, <a href="#CR41" rid="CR41" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459613">

88541</a>

886], and people with PTSD show elevated CB<sub>

8871</sub>

888R availability and reduced peripheral AEA, suggestive of reduced eCB tone [<a href="#CR42" rid="CR42" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459504">

88942</a>

890].</p>

891<p id="__p11">

892Accordingly, CB<sub>

8931</sub>

894R activation has been suggested as a target for anxiolytic drug development [<a href="#CR15" rid="CR15" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459562">

89515</a>

896, <a href="#CR43" rid="CR43" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459557">

89743</a>

898, <a href="#CR44" rid="CR44" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_579828130">

89944</a>

900]. Proposed agents for enhancing CB<sub>

9011</sub>

902R activation include THC, which is a potent and direct agonist; synthetic CB<sub>

9031</sub>

904R agonists; FAAH inhibitors and other agents that increase eCB availability, as well as nonpsychoactive cannabis phytocannabinoids, including CBD. While CBD has low affinity for the CB<sub>

9051</sub>

906R, it functions as an indirect agonist, potentially via augmentation of CB<sub>

9071</sub>

908R constitutional activity, or via increasing AEA through FAAH inhibition (reviewed in [<a href="#CR21" rid="CR21" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459597">

90921</a>

910]).</p>

911<p id="__p12" class="p p-last">

912Several complexities of the eCB system may impact upon the potential of CBD and other CB<sub>

9131</sub>

914R-activating agents to serve as anxiolytic drugs. First, CB<sub>

9151</sub>

916R agonists, including THC and AEA, have a biphasic effect: low doses are anxiolytic, but higher doses are ineffective or anxiogenic, in both preclinical models in and humans (reviewed in [<a href="#CR33" rid="CR33" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459630">

91733</a>

918, <a href="#CR45" rid="CR45" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459503">

91945</a>

920]). This biphasic profile may stem from the capacity of CB<sub>

9211</sub>

922R agonists to also&#x000a0;activate TRPV1 receptors when administered at a high, but not low dose, as demonstrated for AEA [<a href="#CR46" rid="CR46" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459518">

92346</a>

924]. Activation of TRPV1 receptors is predominantly anxiogenic, and thus a critical balance of eCB levels, determining CB1 <em>

925versus</em>

926 TRPV1 activation, is proposed to govern emotional behavior [<a href="#CR27" rid="CR27" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459524">

92727</a>

928, <a href="#CR47" rid="CR47" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459591">

92947</a>

930]. CBD acts as a TRPV1 agonist at high concentrations, potentially by interfering with AEA inactivation [<a href="#CR48" rid="CR48" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459642">

93148</a>

932]. In addition to dose-dependent activation of TRPV1 channels, the anxiogenic <em>

933versus</em>

934 anxiolytic balance of CB<sub>

9351</sub>

936R agonists also depends on dynamic factors, including environmental stressors [<a href="#CR33" rid="CR33" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459530">

93733</a>

938, <a href="#CR49" rid="CR49" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459670">

93949</a>

940].</p>

941</div>

942<div id="Sec6" class="sec sec-last">

943<h3 id="Sec6title">

9445-HT<sub>

9451A</sub>

946 Receptors</h3>

947<p id="__p13" class="p p-first-last">

948The 5-HT<sub>

9491A</sub>

950 receptor (5-HT<sub>

9511A</sub>

952R) is an established anxiolytic target. Buspirone and other 5-HT<sub>

9531A</sub>

954R agonists are approved for the treatment of GAD, with fair response rates [<a href="#CR50" rid="CR50" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_579828126">

95550</a>

956]. In preclinical studies, 5-HT<sub>

9571A</sub>

958R agonists are anxiolytic in animal models of general anxiety [<a href="#CR51" rid="CR51" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459540">

95951</a>

960], prevent the adverse effects of stress [<a href="#CR52" rid="CR52" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459508">

96152</a>

962], and enhance fear extinction [<a href="#CR53" rid="CR53" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459669">

96353</a>

964]. Both pre- and postsynaptic 5-HT<sub>

9651A</sub>

966Rs are coupled to various members of the G<sub>

967i/o</sub>

968 protein family. They are expressed on serotonergic neurons in the raphe, where they exert autoinhibitory function, and various other brain areas involved in fear and anxiety [<a href="#CR54" rid="CR54" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459645">

96954</a>

970, <a href="#CR55" rid="CR55" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459662">

97155</a>

972]. Mechanisms underlying the anxiolytic effects of 5-HT<sub>

9731A</sub>

974R activation are complex, varying between both brain region, and pre- <em>

975versus</em>

976 postsynaptic locus, and are not fully established [<a href="#CR56" rid="CR56" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459649">

97756</a>

978]. While in vitro studies suggest CBD acts as a direct 5-HT<sub>

9791A</sub>

980R agonist [<a href="#CR57" rid="CR57" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459581">

98157</a>

982], in vivo studies are more consistent with CBD acting as an allosteric modulator, or facilitator of 5-HT<sub>

9831A</sub>

984 signaling [<a href="#CR58" rid="CR58" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459651">

98558</a>

986].</p>

987</div>

988</div>

989<div id="Sec7" class="tsec sec">

990<h2 class="head no_bottom_margin" id="Sec7title">

991Preclinical Evaluations</h2>

992<div id="Sec8" class="sec sec-first">

993<h3 id="Sec8title">

994Generalized Anxiety Models</h3>

995<p id="__p14" class="p p-first">

996Relevant studies in animal models are summarized in chronological order in Table <a href="/pmc/articles/PMC4604171/table/Tab1/" target="table" class="fig-table-link figpopup" rid-figpopup="Tab1" rid-ob="ob-Tab1" co-legend-rid="">

997<span style="position: relative;text-decoration:none;">

998&#x200B;<span class="figpopup-sensitive-area" style="left: -2.5em;">

999Table1.</span>

1000</span>

1001<span>

10021</span>

1003</a>

1004. CBD has been studied in a wide range of animal models of general anxiety, including the elevated plus maze (EPM), the Vogel-conflict test (VCT), and the elevated T maze (ETM). See Table <a href="/pmc/articles/PMC4604171/table/Tab1/" target="table" class="fig-table-link figpopup" rid-figpopup="Tab1" rid-ob="ob-Tab1" co-legend-rid="">

1005<span style="position: relative;text-decoration:none;">

1006&#x200B;<span class="figpopup-sensitive-area" style="left: -2.5em;">

1007Table1</span>

1008</span>

1009<span>

10101</span>

1011</a>

1012 for the anxiolytic effect specific to each paradigm. Initial studies of CBD in these models showed conflicting results: high (100&#x000a0;mg/kg) doses&#x000a0;were ineffective, while low (10&#x000a0;mg/kg) doses were anxiolytic [<a href="#CR59" rid="CR59" class=" bibr popnode">

101359</a>

1014, <a href="#CR60" rid="CR60" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459517">

101560</a>

1016]. When tested over a wide range of doses in further studies, the anxiolytic effects of CBD presented a bell-shaped dose&#x02013;response curve, with anxiolytic effects observed at moderate but not higher doses [<a href="#CR61" rid="CR61" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459509">

101761</a>

1018, <a href="#CR90" rid="CR90" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459617">

101990</a>

1020]. All further studies of acute systemic CBD without prior stress showed anxiolytic effects or no effect [<a href="#CR62" rid="CR62" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459507">

102162</a>

1022, <a href="#CR65" rid="CR65" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459521">

102365</a>

1024], the latter study involving intracerebroventricular rather than the intraperitoneal route. No anxiogenic effects of acute systemic CBD dosing in models of general anxiety have yet been reported. As yet, few studies have examined chronic dosing effects of CBD in models of generalized anxiety. Campos et al. [<a href="#CR66" rid="CR66" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459653">

102566</a>

1026] showed that in rat, CBD treatment for 21&#x000a0;days attenuated inhibitory avoidance acquisition [<a href="#CR83" rid="CR83" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459571">

102783</a>

1028]. Long et al. [<a href="#CR69" rid="CR69" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459600">

102969</a>

1030] showed that, in mouse, CBD produced moderate anxiolytic effects in some paradigms, with no effects in others.</p>

1859<div id="largeobj_idm140523329770784" class="largeobj-link align_right" style="display: none">

1860<a target="object" rel="noopener" href="/pmc/articles/PMC4604171/table/Tab1/?report=objectonly">

1861Open in a separate window</a>

1862</div>

1863<div class="tblwrap-foot">

1864<p id="__p16">

1865Effective doses are in bold</p>

1866<p id="__p17">

1867Receptor specific agents: AM251 = cannabinoid receptor type 1 (CB<sub>

18681</sub>

1869R)&#x000a0;inverse agonist; WAY100635 = 5-hydroxytryptamine 1A antagonist; SR141716A = CB<sub>

18701</sub>

1871R antagonist; rimonabant = CB<sub>

18721</sub>

1873R antagonist; capsazepine = transient receptor potential vanilloid type 1 antagonist; naloxone = opioid antagonist; flumazenil =&#x000a0;GABA<sub>

1874A</sub>

1875 receptor antagonist</p>

1876<p id="__p18">

1877Anxiolytic effects in models used: CER = reduced fear response; CFC = reduced conditioned freezing; CFC extinction = reduced freezing following extinction training; EPM = reduced % time in open arm; ETM = decreased inhibitory avoidance; L-DT = increased % time in light; VCT = increased licks indicating reduced conflict; NSF = reduced latency to feed; OF = increased % time in center; SI = increased social interaction</p>

1878<p id="__p19">

1879Anticomplusive effects: MBT = reduced burying</p>

1880<p id="__p20">

1881Panicolytic effects: ETM = decreased escape; GABA<sub>

1882A</sub>

1883 blockade in dlSC = defensive immobility, and explosive escape; PAG-E-Stim = increased threshold for escape; PS = reduced explosive escape</p>

1884<p id="__p21">

1885WR = Wistar rats; SM = Swiss mice; L-E HR = Long&#x02013;Evans hooded rats; i.p. = intraperitoneal; dlPAG = dorsolateral periaqueductal gray; i.c.v. = intracerebroventricular; PL = prelimbic; IL = infralimbic; BNST = bed nucleus of the stria terminalis; CeA = amygdala central nucleus; SNpr = substantia nigra pars reticularis; CUS = chronic unpredictable stress; GSCT = Geller&#x02013;Seifter conflict test; CER = conditioned emotional response; EPM = elevated plus maze; VCT = Vogel conflict test; CFC = contextual fear conditioning; RS = restraint stress; ETM = elevated T maze; PAG E-stim = electrical stimulation of the dlPAG; L-DT = light&#x02013;dark test; SI = social interaction; OF = open field; MBT = marble-burying test; PS = predator stress; NSF = novelty suppressed feeding test; GABA<sub>

1886A</sub>

1887 = &#x003b3;-aminobutyric acid receptor A; dlSC = deep layers superior colliculus; REM = rapid eye movement; NA = not applicable</p>

1888</div>

1889</div>

1890<p id="__p22">

1891Anxiolytic effects of CBD in models of generalized anxiety have been linked to specific receptor mechanisms and brain regions. The midbrain dorsal periaqueductal gray (DPAG) is integral to anxiety, orchestrating autonomic and behavioral responses to threat [<a href="#CR91" rid="CR91" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459551">

189291</a>

1893], and DPAG stimulation in humans produces feelings of intense distress and dread [<a href="#CR92" rid="CR92" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459596">

189492</a>

1895]. Microinjection of CBD into the DPAG produced anxiolytic effects in the EPM, VGC, and ETM that were partially mediated by activation of 5-HT<sub>

18961A</sub>

1897Rs but not by CB<sub>

18981</sub>

1899Rs [<a href="#CR65" rid="CR65" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459661">

190065</a>

1901, <a href="#CR68" rid="CR68" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459510">

190268</a>

1903]. The bed nucleus of the stria terminalis (BNST) serves as a principal output structure of the amygdaloid complex to coordinate sustained fear responses, relevant to anxiety [<a href="#CR93" rid="CR93" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459516">

190493</a>

1905]. Anxiolytic effects of CBD in the EPM and VCT occurred upon microinjection into the BNST, where they depended on 5-HT<sub>

19061A</sub>

1907R activation [<a href="#CR79" rid="CR79" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459650">

190879</a>

1909], and also upon microinjection into the central nucleus of the amygdala [<a href="#CR78" rid="CR78" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459579">

191078</a>

1911]. In the prelimbic cortex, which drives expression of fear responses via connections with the amygdala [<a href="#CR94" rid="CR94" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459538">

191294</a>

1913], CBD had more complex effects: in unstressed rats, CBD was anxiogenic in the EPM, partially via 5-HT<sub>

19141A</sub>

1915R receptor activation; however, following acute restraint stress, CBD was anxiolytic [<a href="#CR87" rid="CR87" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459667">

191687</a>

1917]. Finally, the anxiolytic effects of systemic CBD partially depended on GABA<sub>

1918A</sub>

1919 receptor activation in the EPM model but not in the VCT model [<a href="#CR61" rid="CR61" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459542">

192061</a>

1921, <a href="#CR62" rid="CR62" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459636">

192262</a>

1923].</p>

1924<p id="__p23" class="p p-last">

1925As noted, CBD has been found to have a bell-shaped response curve, with higher doses being ineffective. This may reflect activation of TRPV1 receptors at higher dose, as blockade of TRPV1 receptors in the DPAG rendered a previously ineffective high dose of CBD as anxiolytic in the EPM [<a href="#CR66" rid="CR66" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459531">

192666</a>

1927]. Given TRPV1 receptors have anxiogenic effects, this may indicate that at higher doses, CBD&#x02019;s interaction with TRPV1 receptors to some extent impedes anxiolytic actions, although&#x000a0;was notably not sufficient to produce anxiogenic effects.</p>

1928</div>

1929<div id="Sec9" class="sec">

1930<h3 id="Sec9title">

1931Stress-induced Anxiety Models</h3>

1932<p id="__p24" class="p p-first-last">

1933Stress is an important contributor to anxiety disorders, and traumatic stress exposure is essential to the development of PTSD. Systemically administered CBD reduced acute increases in heart rate and blood pressure induced by restraint stress, as well as the delayed (24&#x000a0;h) anxiogenic effects of stress in the EPM, partially by 5-HT<sub>

19341A</sub>

1935R activation [<a href="#CR67" rid="CR67" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459616">

193667</a>

1937, <a href="#CR73" rid="CR73" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459546">

193873</a>

1939]. However intra-BNST microinjection of CBD <em>

1940augmented</em>

1941 stress-induced heart rate increase, also partially via 5-HT<sub>

19421A</sub>

1943R activation [<a href="#CR85" rid="CR85" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459668">

194485</a>

1945]. In a subchronic study, CBD administered daily 1&#x000a0;h after predator stress (a proposed model of PTSD) reduced the long-lasting anxiogenic effects of chronic predator stress, partially via 5-HT<sub>

19461A</sub>

1947R activation [<a href="#CR77" rid="CR77" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459614">

194877</a>

1949]. In a chronic study, systemic CBD prevented increased anxiety produced by chronic unpredictable stress, in addition to increasing hippocampal AEA; these anxiolytic effects depended upon CB<sub>

19501</sub>

1951R activation and hippocampal neurogenesis, as demonstrated by genetic ablation techniques [<a href="#CR81" rid="CR81" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459629">

195281</a>

1953]. Prior stress also appears to <em>

1954modulate</em>

1955 CBD&#x02019;s anxiogenic effects: microinjection of CBD into the prelimbic cortex of unstressed animals was anxiogenic in the EPM but following restraint stress was found to be anxiolytic [<a href="#CR87" rid="CR87" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459574">

195687</a>

1957]. Likewise, systemic CBD was anxiolytic in the EPM following but not prior to stress [<a href="#CR65" rid="CR65" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459605">

195865</a>

1959].</p>

1960</div>

1961<div id="Sec10" class="sec">

1962<h3 id="Sec10title">

1963PD and Compulsive Behavior Models</h3>

1964<p id="__p25" class="p p-first-last">

1965CBD inhibited escape responses in the ETM and increased DPAG escape electrical threshold [<a href="#CR68" rid="CR68" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459610">

196668</a>

1967], both proposed models of panic attacks [<a href="#CR95" rid="CR95" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459619">

196895</a>

1969]. These effects partially depended on 5-HT<sub>

19701A</sub>

1971R activation but were not affected by CB<sub>

19721</sub>

1973R blockade. CBD was also panicolytic in the predator&#x02013;prey model, which assesses explosive escape and defensive immobility in response to a boa constrictor snake, also partially via 5-HT<sub>

19741A</sub>

1975R activation; however, more consistent with an anxiogenic effect, CBD was also noted to decrease time spent outside the burrow and increase defensive attention (not shown in Table <a href="/pmc/articles/PMC4604171/table/Tab1/" target="table" class="fig-table-link figpopup" rid-figpopup="Tab1" rid-ob="ob-Tab1" co-legend-rid="">

1976<span style="position: relative;text-decoration:none;">

1977&#x200B;<span class="figpopup-sensitive-area" style="left: -2.5em;">

1978Table1)</span>

1979</span>

1980<span>

19811</span>

1982</a>

1983) [<a href="#CR75" rid="CR75" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459563">

198475</a>

1985, <a href="#CR86" rid="CR86" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459621">

198686</a>

1987] . Finally, CBD, partially via CB<sub>

19881</sub>

1989Rs, decreased defensive immobility and explosive escape caused by bicuculline-induced neuronal activation in the superior colliculus [<a href="#CR89" rid="CR89" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459564">

199089</a>

1991]. Anticompulsive effects of CBD were investigated in marble-burying behavior, conceptualized to model OCD [<a href="#CR96" rid="CR96" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459511">

199296</a>

1993]. Acute systemic CBD reduced marble-burying behavior for up to 7&#x000a0;days, with no attenuation in effect up to high (120&#x000a0;mg/kg) doses, and effect shown to depend on CB<sub>

19941</sub>

1995Rs but not 5-HT<sub>

19961A</sub>

1997Rs [<a href="#CR71" rid="CR71" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459594">

199871</a>

1999, <a href="#CR74" rid="CR74" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459560">

200074</a>

2001, <a href="#CR88" rid="CR88" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459627">

200288</a>

2003].</p>

2004</div>

2005<div id="Sec11" class="sec">

2006<h3 id="Sec11title">

2007Contextual Fear Conditioning, Fear Extinction, and Reconsolidation Blockade</h3>

2008<p id="__p26" class="p p-first">

2009Several studies assessed CBD using contextual fear conditioning. Briefly, this paradigm involves pairing a neutral context, the conditioned stimulus (CS), with an aversive unconditioned stimulus (US), a mild foot shock. After repeated pairings, the subject learns that the CS predicts the US, and subsequent CS presentation elicits freezing and other physiological responses. Systemic administration of CBD prior to CS re-exposure reduced conditioned cardiovascular responses [<a href="#CR63" rid="CR63" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459639">

201063</a>

2011], an effect reproduced by microinjection of CBD into the BNST, and partially mediated by 5-HT<sub>

20121A</sub>

2013R activation [<a href="#CR79" rid="CR79" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459657">

201479</a>

2015]. Similarly, CBD in the prelimbic cortex reduced conditioned freezing [<a href="#CR70" rid="CR70" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459635">

201670</a>

2017], an effect prevented by 5-HT<sub>

20181A</sub>

2019R blockade [<a href="#CR87" rid="CR87" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459590">

202087</a>

2021]. By contrast, CBD microinjection in the infralimbic cortex <em>

2022enhanced</em>

2023 conditioned freezing [<a href="#CR70" rid="CR70" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459643">

202470</a>

2025]. Finally, El Batsh et al. [<a href="#CR80" rid="CR80" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459577">

202680</a>

2027] reported that repeated CBD doses over 21&#x000a0;days, that is chronic as opposed to acute treatment, <em>

2028facilitated</em>

2029 conditioned freezing. In this study, CBD was administered prior to conditioning rather than prior to re-exposure as in acute studies, thus further directly comparable studies are required.</p>

2030<p id="__p27">

2031CBD has also been shown to enhance extinction of contextually conditioned fear responses. Extinction training involves repeated CS exposure in the absence of the US, leading to the formation of a new memory that inhibits fear responses and a decline in freezing over subsequent training sessions. Systemic CBD administration immediately before training markedly enhanced extinction, and this effect depended on CB<sub>

20321</sub>

2033R activation, without involvement of TRPV1 receptors [<a href="#CR65" rid="CR65" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459582">

203465</a>

2035]. Further studies showed CB<sub>

20361</sub>

2037Rs in the infralimbic cortex may be involved in this effect [<a href="#CR82" rid="CR82" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459533">

203882</a>

2039].</p>

2040<p id="__p28" class="p p-last">

2041CBD also blocked reconsolidation of aversive memories in rat [<a href="#CR76" rid="CR76" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459672">

204276</a>

2043]. Briefly, fear memories, when reactivated by re-exposure (retrieval), enter into a labile state in which the memory trace may either be reconsolidated or extinguished [<a href="#CR97" rid="CR97" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_820187857">

204497</a>

2045], and this process may be pharmacologically modulated to achieve reconsolidation blockade or extinction. When administered immediately following retrieval, CBD prevented freezing to the conditioned context upon further re-exposure, and no reinstatement or spontaneous recovery was observed over 3&#x000a0;weeks, consistent with reconsolidation blockade rather than extinction [<a href="#CR76" rid="CR76" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459624">

204676</a>

2047]. This effect depended on CB<sub>

20481</sub>

2049R activation but not 5-HT<sub>

20501A</sub>

2051R activation [<a href="#CR76" rid="CR76" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459586">

205276</a>

2053].</p>

2054</div>

2055<div id="Sec12" class="sec sec-last">

2056<h3 id="Sec12title">

2057Summary and Clinical Relevance</h3>

2058<p id="__p29" class="p p-first">

2059Overall, existing preclinical evidence strongly supports the potential of CBD as a treatment for anxiety disorders. CBD exhibits a broad range of actions, relevant to multiple symptom domains, including anxiolytic, panicolytic, and anticompulsive actions, as well as a decrease in autonomic arousal, a decrease in&#x000a0;conditioned fear expression, enhancement of fear extinction, reconsolidation blockade, and prevention of the long-term anxiogenic effects of stress. Activation of 5-HT<sub>

20601A</sub>

2061Rs appears to mediate anxiolytic and panicolytic effects, in addition to reducing conditioned fear expression, although CB<sub>

20621</sub>

2063R activation may play a limited role. By contrast, CB<sub>

20641</sub>

2065R activation appears to mediate CBD&#x02019;s anticompulsive effects, enhancement of fear extinction, reconsolidation blockade, and capacity to prevent the long-term anxiogenic consequences of stress, with involvement of hippocampal neurogenesis.</p>

2066<p id="__p30">

2067While CBD predominantly has acute anxiolytic effects, some species discrepancies are apparent. In addition, effects may be contingent on prior stress and vary according to brain region. A notable contrast between CBD and other agents that target the eCB system, including THC, direct CB<sub>

20681</sub>

2069R agonists and FAAH inhibitors, is a lack of anxiogenic effects at a higher dose. Further receptor-specific studies may elucidate the receptor specific basis of this distinct dose response profile. Further studies are also required to establish the efficacy of CBD when administered in chronic dosing, as relatively few relevant studies exist, with mixed results, including both anxiolytic and anxiogenic outcomes.</p>

2070<p id="__p31" class="p p-last">

2071Overall, preclinical evidence supports systemic CBD as an acute treatment of GAD, SAD, PD, OCD, and PTSD,&#x000a0;and suggests that CBD has the advantage of not producing anxiogenic effects at higher dose, as distinct from other agents that enhance CB<sub>

20721</sub>

2073R&#x000a0;activation. In particular, results show potential for the treatment of multiple PTSD symptom domains, including reducing arousal and avoidance, preventing the long-term adverse effects of stress, as well as enhancing the extinction and blocking the reconsolidation of persistent fear memories.</p>

2074</div>

2075</div>

2076<div id="Sec13" class="tsec sec">

2077<h2 class="head no_bottom_margin" id="Sec13title">

2078Human Experimental and Clinical Studies</h2>

2079<div id="Sec14" class="sec sec-first">

2080<h3 id="Sec14title">

2081Evidence from Acute Psychological Studies</h3>

2082<p id="__p32" class="p p-first-last">

2083Relevant studies are summarized in Table <a href="/pmc/articles/PMC4604171/table/Tab2/" target="table" class="fig-table-link figpopup" rid-figpopup="Tab2" rid-ob="ob-Tab2" co-legend-rid="">

2084<span style="position: relative;text-decoration:none;">

2085&#x200B;<span class="figpopup-sensitive-area" style="left: -2.5em;">

2086Table2.</span>

2087</span>

2088<span>

20892</span>

2090</a>

2091. The anxiolytic effects of CBD in humans were first demonstrated in the context of reversing the anxiogenic effects of THC. CBD reduced THC-induced anxiety when administered simultaneously with this agent, but had no effect on baseline anxiety when administered alone [<a href="#CR99" rid="CR99" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459525">

209299</a>

2093, <a href="#CR100" rid="CR100" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459603">

2094100</a>

2095]. Further studies using higher doses supported a lack of anxiolytic effects at baseline [<a href="#CR101" rid="CR101" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_579828129">

2096101</a>

2097, <a href="#CR107" rid="CR107" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459569">

2098107</a>

2099]. By contrast, CBD potently reduces experimentally induced anxiety or fear. CBD reduced anxiety associated with a simulated public speaking test in healthy subjects, and in subjects with SAD, showing a comparable efficacy to ipsapirone (a 5-HT<sub>

21001A</sub>

2101R agonist) or diazepam [<a href="#CR98" rid="CR98" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459544">

210298</a>

2103, <a href="#CR105" rid="CR105" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459565">

2104105</a>

2105]. CBD also reduced the presumed anticipatory anxiety associated with undergoing a single-photon emission computed tomography (SPECT) imaging procedure, in both healthy and SAD subjects [<a href="#CR102" rid="CR102" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459584">

2106102</a>

2107, <a href="#CR104" rid="CR104" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459514">

2108104</a>

2109]. Finally, CBD enhanced extinction of fear memories in healthy volunteers: specifically, inhaled CBD administered prior to or after extinction training in a contextual fear conditioning paradigm led to a trend-level enhancement in the reduction of skin conductance response during reinstatement, and a significant reduction in expectancy (of shock) ratings during reinstatement [<a href="#CR106" rid="CR106" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459606">

2110106</a>

2111].</p>

2318<h3 id="Sec15title">

2319Evidence from Neuroimaging Studies</h3>

2320<p id="__p35" class="p p-first">

2321Relevant studies are summarized in Table <a href="/pmc/articles/PMC4604171/table/Tab3/" target="table" class="fig-table-link figpopup" rid-figpopup="Tab3" rid-ob="ob-Tab3" co-legend-rid="">

2322<span style="position: relative;text-decoration:none;">

2323&#x200B;<span class="figpopup-sensitive-area" style="left: -2.5em;">

2324Table3.</span>

2325</span>

2326<span>

23273</span>

2328</a>

2329. In a SPECT study of resting cerebral blood flow (rCBF) in normal subjects, CBD reduced rCBF in left medial temporal areas, including the amygdala and hippocampus, as well as the hypothalamus and left posterior cingulate gyrus, but increased rCBF in the left parahippocampal gyrus. These rCBF changes were not correlated with anxiolytic effects [<a href="#CR102" rid="CR102" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459675">

2330102</a>

2332104</a>

2333].</p>

2403Oral 600&#x000a0;mg,<br />

2404acute, 1&#x02013;2&#x000a0;h</td>

2405<td rowspan="1" colspan="1">

2406fMRI activation during fearful faces task</td>

2407<td rowspan="1" colspan="1">

2408&#x02193; Activation in left medial temporal region, including amygdala and anterior PHG, and in right ACC and PCC</td>

2409</tr>

2410<tr>

2411<td rowspan="1" colspan="1">

2412Fusar-Poli et al. [<a href="#CR110" rid="CR110" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459663">

2413110</a>

2414]</td>

2415<td rowspan="1" colspan="1">

241615&#x000a0;HV,<br />

2417DBP</td>

2418<td rowspan="1" colspan="1">

2420acute, 1&#x02013;2&#x000a0;h</td>

2422fMRI functional connectivity during fearful faces task</td>

2423<td rowspan="1" colspan="1">

2424&#x02193; Functional connectivity between L) AMY and ACC</td>

2425</tr>

2426<tr>

2428Crippa et al. [<a href="#CR104" rid="CR104" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459623">

2429104</a>

2433DBP</td>

2435Oral 400&#x000a0;mg,<br />

2436acute, 75 and 140&#x000a0;min</td>

2438SPECT, resting (rCBF)</td>

2439<td rowspan="1" colspan="1">

2440&#x02193; rCBF in the left PHG, HPC and ITG.<br />

2441&#x02191; rCBF in the right posterior cingulate gyrus</td>

2442</tr>

2443</tbody>

2444</table>

2445</div>

2446<div id="largeobj_idm140523345356176" class="largeobj-link align_right" style="display: none">

2447<a target="object" rel="noopener" href="/pmc/articles/PMC4604171/table/Tab3/?report=objectonly">

2448Open in a separate window</a>

2449</div>

2450<div class="tblwrap-foot">

2451<p id="__p37">

2452CBD = cannabidiol; HV = healthy controls; DBP = double-blind placebo; SAD = social anxiety disorder; HC = healthy controls; SPECT = single-photo emission computed tomography; rCBF = regional cerebral blood flow; fMRI = functional magnetic resonance imaging; HPC = hippocampus; HYP = hypothalamus; PHG = parahippocampal gyrus; STG = superior temporal gyrus; MTG = medial temporal gyrus; ACC = anterior cingulate cortex; PCC = posterior cingulate cortex</p>

2453</div>

2454</div>

2455<p id="__p38" class="p p-last">

2456In a series of placebo-controlled studies involving 15 healthy volunteers, Fusar-Poli et al. investigated the effects of CBD and THC on task-related blood-oxygen-level dependent functional magnetic resonance imaging activation, specifically the go/no-go and fearful faces tasks [<a href="#CR109" rid="CR109" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_579828124">

2457109</a>

2458, <a href="#CR110" rid="CR110" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459655">

2459110</a>

2460]. The go/no-go task measures response inhibition, and is associated with activation of medial prefrontal, dorsolateral prefrontal, and parietal areas [<a href="#CR111" rid="CR111" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_579828132">

2461111</a>

2462]. Response activation is diminished in PTSD and other anxiety disorders, and increased activation predicts response to treatment [<a href="#CR112" rid="CR112" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459543">

2463112</a>

2464]. CBD produced no changes in predicted areas (relative to placebo) but reduced activation in the left insula, superior temporal gyrus, and transverse temporal gyrus. The fearful faces task activates the amygdala, and other medial temporal areas involved in emotion processing, and heightened amygdala response activation has been reported in anxiety disorders, including GAD and PTSD [<a href="#CR113" rid="CR113" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459625">

2465113</a>

2466, <a href="#CR114" rid="CR114" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459604">

2467114</a>

2468]. CBD attenuated blood-oxygen-level dependent activation in the left amygdala, and the anterior and posterior cingulate cortex in response to intensely fearful faces, and also reduced amplitude in skin conductance fluctuation, which was highly correlated with amygdala activation [<a href="#CR109" rid="CR109" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_579828122">

2469109</a>

2470]. Dynamic causal modeling analysis in this data set further showed CBD reduced forward functional connectivity between the amygdala and anterior cingulate cortex [<a href="#CR110" rid="CR110" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459572">

2471110</a>

2472].</p>

2473</div>

2474<div id="Sec16" class="sec">

2475<h3 id="Sec16title">

2476Evidence from Epidemiological and Chronic Studies</h3>

2477<p id="__p39" class="p p-first-last">

2478Epidemiological studies of various neuropsychiatric disorders indicate that a higher CBD content in chronically consumed cannabis may protect against adverse effects of THC, including psychotic symptoms, drug cravings, memory loss, and hippocampal gray matter loss [<a href="#CR115" rid="CR115" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459598">

2479115</a>

2480&#x02013;<a href="#CR118" rid="CR118" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459535">

2481118</a>

2482] (reviewed in [<a href="#CR119" rid="CR119" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459626">

2483119</a>

2484]). As THC acutely induces anxiety, this pattern may also be evident for chronic anxiety symptoms. Two studies were identified, including an uncontrolled retrospective study in civilian patients with PTSD patients [<a href="#CR120" rid="CR120" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459637">

2485120</a>

2486], and a case study in a patient with severe sexual abuse-related PTSD [<a href="#CR121" rid="CR121" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_555459545">

2487121</a>

2488], which showed that chronic cannabis use significantly reduces PTSD symptoms; however, these studies did not include data on the THC:CBD ratio. Thus, overall, no outcome data are currently available regarding the chronic effects of CBD in the treatment of anxiety symptoms, nor do any data exist regarding the potential protective effects of CBD on anxiety potentially induced by chronic THC use.</p>

2489</div>

2490<div id="Sec17" class="sec sec-last">

2491<h3 id="Sec17title">

2492Summary and Clinical Relevance</h3>

2493<p id="__p40" class="p p-first-last">

2494Evidence from human studies strongly supports the potential for CBD as a treatment for anxiety disorders: at oral doses ranging from 300 to 600&#x000a0;mg, CBD reduces experimentally induced anxiety in healthy controls, without affecting baseline anxiety levels, and reduces anxiety in patients with SAD. Limited results in healthy subjects also support the efficacy of CBD in acutely enhancing fear extinction, suggesting potential for the treatment of PTSD, or for enhancing cognitive behavioral therapy. Neuroimaging findings provide evidence of neurobiological targets that may underlie CBD&#x02019;s anxiolytic effects, including reduced amygdala activation and altered medial prefrontal amygdala connectivity, although current findings&#x000a0;are limited by&#x000a0;small sample sizes, and a lack of independent replication. Further studies are also required to establish whether chronic, in addition to acute CBD dosing is anxiolytic in human. Also, clinical findings are currently limited to SAD, whereas preclinical evidence suggests CBD&#x02019;s potential to treat multiple symptom domains relevant to GAD, PD, and, particularly, PTSD.</p>

2495</div>

2496</div>

2497<div id="Sec18" class="tsec sec">

2498<h2 class="head no_bottom_margin" id="Sec18title">

2499Conclusions</h2>

2500<p id="__p41" class="p p-first-last">

2501Preclinical evidence conclusively demonstrates CBD&#x02019;s efficacy in reducing anxiety behaviors relevant to multiple disorders, including PTSD, GAD, PD, OCD, and SAD, with a notable lack of anxiogenic effects. CBD&#x02019;s anxiolytic actions appear to depend upon CB<sub>

25021</sub>

2503Rs and 5-HT<sub>

25041A</sub>

2505Rs in several brain regions; however, investigation of additional receptor actions may reveal further mechanisms. Human experimental findings support preclinical findings, and also suggest a lack of anxiogenic effects, minimal sedative effects, and an excellent safety profile. Current preclinical and human findings mostly involve acute CBD dosing&#x000a0;in healthy subjects, so further studies are required to establish whether chronic dosing of CBD has similar effects in relevant clinical populations. Overall, this review emphasizes the potential value and need for further study of CBD in the treatment of anxiety disorders.</p>

2506</div>
